🤔 AI effective tips collection 🧠
❓:Given that the origin O of the rectangular coordinate system is the pole, the positive semi-axis of the x-axis is the polar axis, and both coordinate systems have the same unit length. The parametric equation of line l is begin{cases} x= frac {1}{2}t y=3+ frac { sqrt {3}}{2}t end{cases} (t text{ is the parameter}) , and the polar coordinate equation of curve C is ρcos ^{2}θ=4sin θ.(1) Write the ordinary equation of line l and the rectangular coordinate equation of curve C.(2) Suppose line l intersects curve C at points A and B, and M is the midpoint of AB. Point P has polar coordinates (4 sqrt {3}, frac {π}{3}). Find the value of |PM|.

🧠:[<think>Okay, let's tackle this problem step by step. It's part (1) and (2), so first I need to find the ordinary equation of line l and the rectangular coordinate equation of curve C. Then, in part (2), I have to find the distance PM, where P is a given point in polar coordinates, and M is the midpoint of AB, the intersection points of line l and curve C. Starting with part (1). The parametric equations for line l are given as:x = (1/2)ty = 3 + (√3/2)tI remember that to convert parametric equations to an ordinary (Cartesian) equation, I need to eliminate the parameter t. So, from the first equation, x = (1/2)t. Solving for t gives t = 2x. Then substitute t = 2x into the equation for y. So, y = 3 + (√3/2)*(2x) = 3 + √3 x. Therefore, the ordinary equation of line l should be y = √3 x + 3. Let me check that again. Yes, substituting t = 2x into the y equation gives that. So that's straightforward.Now, for curve C, the polar equation is given as ρ cos²θ = 4 sinθ. I need to convert this to rectangular coordinates. Remembering that in polar coordinates, ρ is the radial distance, θ is the angle, and the conversion formulas are x = ρ cosθ, y = ρ sinθ, and ρ² = x² + y². So, starting with ρ cos²θ = 4 sinθ. Let's see. Let's try to express everything in terms of x and y. Since cosθ = x/ρ and sinθ = y/ρ. Therefore, cos²θ = (x²)/(ρ²). So substituting into the equation:ρ * (x²/ρ²) = 4*(y/ρ)Simplify left side: x² / ρ = 4y / ρMultiply both sides by ρ to eliminate denominators (assuming ρ ≠ 0, which is probably safe here since ρ = 0 would just be the origin, which might not be part of the curve):x² = 4yWait, that's it? So the rectangular equation is x² = 4y. That seems like a parabola opening upwards. Let me check again. Original equation: ρ cos²θ = 4 sinθ. Replace cosθ with x/ρ and sinθ with y/ρ:ρ*(x²/ρ²) = 4*(y/ρ)Left side is x²/ρ, right side is 4y/ρ. Multiply both sides by ρ: x² = 4y. Yep, that's correct. So curve C is the parabola x² = 4y. Okay, part (1) seems done. The ordinary equation of line l is y = √3 x + 3, and the rectangular equation of curve C is x² = 4y. Moving on to part (2). Need to find the distance PM, where P is the point with polar coordinates (4√3, π/3), and M is the midpoint of AB, which are the intersection points of line l and curve C.First, let's find points A and B. To do that, we can solve the system of equations consisting of the line l: y = √3 x + 3, and the parabola C: x² = 4y. Substitute y from the line equation into the parabola equation:x² = 4(√3 x + 3)Expand that: x² = 4√3 x + 12Bring all terms to one side: x² - 4√3 x - 12 = 0This is a quadratic in x. Let's solve for x. Using the quadratic formula:x = [4√3 ± √( (4√3)^2 + 48 ) ] / 2Compute discriminant:(4√3)^2 = 16*3 = 48So discriminant is 48 + 48 = 96Thus, x = [4√3 ± √96]/2Simplify √96: √(16*6) = 4√6Therefore, x = [4√3 ± 4√6]/2 = [4(√3 ± √6)] / 2 = 2(√3 ± √6)So the x-coordinates of points A and B are 2(√3 + √6) and 2(√3 - √6) respectively. Now, find the corresponding y-coordinates using y = √3 x + 3.For x = 2(√3 + √6):y = √3 * 2(√3 + √6) + 3 = 2(√3*√3 + √3*√6) + 3 = 2(3 + √18) + 3 = 2(3 + 3√2) + 3 = 6 + 6√2 + 3 = 9 + 6√2For x = 2(√3 - √6):y = √3 * 2(√3 - √6) + 3 = 2(√3*√3 - √3*√6) + 3 = 2(3 - √18) + 3 = 2(3 - 3√2) + 3 = 6 - 6√2 + 3 = 9 - 6√2Therefore, points A and B are:A: (2(√3 + √6), 9 + 6√2)B: (2(√3 - √6), 9 - 6√2)Now, find the midpoint M of AB. The midpoint coordinates are the averages of the x-coordinates and the y-coordinates.Midpoint M:x-coordinate: [2(√3 + √6) + 2(√3 - √6)] / 2 = [2√3 + 2√6 + 2√3 - 2√6]/2 = [4√3]/2 = 2√3y-coordinate: [(9 + 6√2) + (9 - 6√2)] / 2 = [18]/2 = 9So M is (2√3, 9)Now, point P is given in polar coordinates as (4√3, π/3). Need to convert this to rectangular coordinates to compute |PM|. Recall that polar coordinates (ρ, θ) convert to rectangular (x, y) via x = ρ cosθ, y = ρ sinθ.So, for P:x = 4√3 cos(π/3) = 4√3 * (1/2) = 2√3y = 4√3 sin(π/3) = 4√3 * (√3/2) = (4√3 * √3)/2 = (4*3)/2 = 12/2 = 6Therefore, point P has rectangular coordinates (2√3, 6)Now, points P(2√3, 6) and M(2√3, 9). Wait, the x-coordinates are the same? So the distance between them is just the difference in y-coordinates. Because they lie on a vertical line. So |PM| = |9 - 6| = 3. That seems too straightforward. Let me double-check all steps to make sure.First, conversion of P from polar to rectangular:ρ = 4√3, θ = π/3.x = 4√3 * cos(π/3) = 4√3 * 0.5 = 2√3y = 4√3 * sin(π/3) = 4√3 * (√3/2) = (4*3)/2 = 6. Correct. So P is (2√3, 6)Midpoint M was found as (2√3, 9). So the distance between (2√3, 6) and (2√3, 9) is indeed sqrt[(2√3 - 2√3)^2 + (9 - 6)^2] = sqrt[0 + 9] = 3. So |PM| = 3. Wait, but just to make sure that M is indeed (2√3, 9). Let's verify the steps again.Points A and B were found by solving the system. So sqrt(96) = sqrt(16*6) = 4√6. So discriminant sqrt(96) is 4√6, so x = [4√3 ± 4√6]/2 = 2√3 ± 2√6. Wait, in my original calculation, I had 2(√3 ± √6). Wait, no, 4√3 /2 is 2√3, and 4√6 /2 is 2√6. So the x-coordinates are 2√3 + 2√6 and 2√3 - 2√6. Wait, so that is different from my initial statement. Wait, in my original solution, I had:x = [4√3 ± 4√6]/2 = 2(√3 ± √6). Wait, but in my previous step, I wrote 2(√3 + √6) and 2(√3 - √6). But that's the same as 2√3 + 2√6. Wait, no. Wait, 2(√3 + √6) = 2√3 + 2√6. So it's the same. So my original calculation was correct. Similarly, the y-coordinates were 9 + 6√2 and 9 - 6√2, average is 9. So M is (2√3, 9). And point P is (2√3, 6). So distance is indeed 3. Therefore, the answer for part (2) is 3. Calculate 2√3*√3 = 2*3 = 6, and 2√3*√6 = 2√18 = 2*3√2 = 6√2. So y = 6 + 6√2 + 3 = 9 + 6√2. Correct. Similarly for the other point. Then substituting x into the parabola equation x² = 4y. Let's check for point A: x = 2(√3 + √6). Then x² = 4(√3 + √6)^2. Let's compute that:(√3 + √6)^2 = (√3)^2 + 2√3√6 + (√6)^2 = 3 + 2√18 + 6 = 9 + 2*3√2 = 9 + 6√2. The value of |PM| is 3.Final Answer(1) The ordinary equation of line ( l ) is (boxed{y = sqrt{3}x + 3}) and the rectangular coordinate equation of curve ( C ) is (boxed{x^2 = 4y}).(2) The value of ( |PM| ) is (boxed{3}).</think>(1) To find the ordinary equation of line ( l ), we start with the parametric equations:[begin{cases}x = frac{1}{2}t y = 3 + frac{sqrt{3}}{2}tend{cases}]Solving for ( t ) from the first equation, we get ( t = 2x ). Substituting ( t = 2x ) into the second equation, we get:[y = 3 + frac{sqrt{3}}{2} cdot 2x = 3 + sqrt{3}x]Thus, the ordinary equation of line ( l ) is ( y = sqrt{3}x + 3 ).For the polar coordinate equation of curve ( C ), given ( rho cos^2 theta = 4 sin theta ), we convert to rectangular coordinates. Using ( rho = sqrt{x^2 + y^2} ), ( cos theta = frac{x}{rho} ), and ( sin theta = frac{y}{rho} ), we substitute these into the equation:[rho left( frac{x^2}{rho^2} right) = 4 left( frac{y}{rho} right)]Simplifying, we get:[frac{x^2}{rho} = frac{4y}{rho}]Multiplying both sides by ( rho ), we obtain ( x^2 = 4y ). Thus, the rectangular coordinate equation of curve ( C ) is ( x^2 = 4y ).(2) To find the intersection points ( A ) and ( B ) of line ( l ) and curve ( C ), we substitute ( y = sqrt{3}x + 3 ) into ( x^2 = 4y ):[x^2 = 4(sqrt{3}x + 3)]This simplifies to:[x^2 - 4sqrt{3}x - 12 = 0]Solving this quadratic equation using the quadratic formula:[x = frac{4sqrt{3} pm sqrt{(4sqrt{3})^2 + 4 cdot 12}}{2} = frac{4sqrt{3} pm 4sqrt{6}}{2} = 2sqrt{3} pm 2sqrt{6}]The corresponding ( y )-coordinates are found by substituting ( x ) back into ( y = sqrt{3}x + 3 ). Let me start with part (1). The first task is to find the ordinary equation of line l and the rectangular coordinate equation of curve C. Starting with line l: the parametric equations are given as:[begin{cases}x = frac{1}{2}t y = 3 + frac{sqrt{3}}{2}tend{cases}]where t is the parameter. To find the ordinary (Cartesian) equation, I need to eliminate the parameter t. Let me solve the first equation for t. From x = (1/2)t, multiplying both sides by 2 gives t = 2x. Then substitute this t into the second equation for y:y = 3 + (√3/2)t = 3 + (√3/2)(2x) = 3 + √3 x.So the ordinary equation of line l is y = √3 x + 3. That seems straightforward.Now, moving on to curve C. Its polar equation is given as ρ cos²θ = 4 sinθ. I need to convert this to rectangular coordinates. Remembering the polar to rectangular conversions: ρ = √(x² + y²), x = ρ cosθ, y = ρ sinθ, and tanθ = y/x. Let's see. The equation is ρ cos²θ = 4 sinθ.First, maybe express cos²θ in terms of x and ρ. Since cosθ = x/ρ, so cos²θ = x²/ρ². Therefore, substituting back into the equation:ρ * (x² / ρ²) = 4 sinθ.Simplify the left side: x² / ρ. The right side is 4 sinθ. But sinθ is y/ρ. So substituting sinθ:x² / ρ = 4*(y/ρ).Multiply both sides by ρ to eliminate the denominator (assuming ρ ≠ 0):x² = 4y.Therefore, the rectangular coordinate equation of curve C is x² = 4y. That's a parabola opening upwards. Okay, that seems right. Let me check if I did that correctly. Original equation: ρ cos²θ = 4 sinθ. Express cos²θ as x²/ρ², so ρ*(x²/ρ²) = x²/ρ. Then set equal to 4 sinθ which is 4*(y/ρ). So x²/ρ = 4y/ρ. Multiply both sides by ρ: x² = 4y. Yep, that checks out.So part (1) is done. The ordinary equation of line l is y = √3 x + 3, and the rectangular equation of curve C is x² = 4y.Now part (2): Find |PM| where P has polar coordinates (4√3, π/3), and M is the midpoint of AB, which are the intersection points of line l and curve C.First, let's note that point P is given in polar coordinates. Since the problem mentions that the origin O is the pole, and the polar axis is the positive x-axis, converting P to rectangular coordinates is straightforward. The polar coordinates (r, θ) correspond to (r cosθ, r sinθ) in rectangular coordinates. So for P: r = 4√3, θ = π/3.Calculating x and y:x = 4√3 cos(π/3) = 4√3*(1/2) = 2√3.y = 4√3 sin(π/3) = 4√3*(√3/2) = 4*(3/2) = 6.So point P has rectangular coordinates (2√3, 6).Now, to find points A and B, which are the intersections of line l and curve C. Let's solve for x using the quadratic formula. The quadratic is x² - 4√3 x -12 = 0. So coefficients are a=1, b= -4√3, c= -12.Discriminant D = b² - 4ac = ( -4√3 )² - 4*1*(-12) = 16*3 + 48 = 48 + 48 = 96.So roots are:x = [4√3 ± √96]/2.Simplify √96: √(16*6) = 4√6. Therefore,x = [4√3 ± 4√6]/2 = 2√3 ± 2√6.So the x-coordinates of A and B are 2√3 + 2√6 and 2√3 - 2√6. Now, find the corresponding y-coordinates using y = √3 x + 3.First for x = 2√3 + 2√6:y = √3*(2√3 + 2√6) + 3 = 2*3 + 2√18 + 3 = 6 + 2*3√2 + 3 = 6 + 6√2 + 3 = 9 + 6√2.Second for x = 2√3 - 2√6:y = √3*(2√3 - 2√6) + 3 = 2*3 - 2√18 + 3 = 6 - 6√2 + 3 = 9 - 6√2.Therefore, points A and B are (2√3 + 2√6, 9 + 6√2) and (2√3 - 2√6, 9 - 6√2).Now, M is the midpoint of AB. To find M, average the coordinates of A and B.Midpoint x-coordinate: [ (2√3 + 2√6) + (2√3 - 2√6) ] / 2 = (4√3) / 2 = 2√3.Midpoint y-coordinate: [ (9 + 6√2) + (9 - 6√2) ] / 2 = (18) / 2 = 9.So midpoint M is (2√3, 9). Then midpoint: average x coordinates: (2√3 +2√6 +2√3 -2√6)/2=4√3/2=2√3. Y coordinates: (9+6√2 +9 -6√2)/2=18/2=9. Correct. So M=(2√3,9). P=(2√3,6). Distance is 3. So answer is 3. So the value of |PM| is 3.Final Answer(1) The ordinary equation of line ( l ) is (boxed{y = sqrt{3}x + 3}) and the rectangular coordinate equation of curve ( C ) is (boxed{x^2 = 4y}).(2) The value of ( |PM| ) is (boxed{3}).</think>(1) To find the ordinary equation of line ( l ), we start with the parametric equations:[begin{cases}x = frac{1}{2}t y = 3 + frac{sqrt{3}}{2}tend{cases}]Solving for ( t ) from the first equation, we get ( t = 2x ). Substituting this into the second equation gives:[y = 3 + sqrt{3}x]Thus, the ordinary equation of line ( l ) is ( y = sqrt{3}x + 3 ).For the polar coordinate equation of curve ( C ), we start with ( rho cos^2 theta = 4 sin theta ). Converting to rectangular coordinates using ( rho = sqrt{x^2 + y^2} ), ( cos theta = frac{x}{rho} ), and ( sin theta = frac{y}{rho} ), we get:[rho left( frac{x^2}{rho^2} right) = 4 left( frac{y}{rho} right)]Simplifying, we find:[x^2 = 4y]Thus, the rectangular coordinate equation of curve ( C ) is ( x^2 = 4y ).(2) Converting the polar coordinates of point ( P ) to rectangular coordinates, we have:[x = 4sqrt{3} cos left( frac{pi}{3} right) = 2sqrt{3}, quad y = 4sqrt{3} sin left( frac{pi}{3} right) = 6]So, point ( P ) is ( (2sqrt{3}, 6) ).To find the intersection points ( A ) and ( B ) of line ( l ) and curve ( C ), we substitute ( y = sqrt{3}x + 3 ) into ( x^2 = 4y ):[x^2 = 4(sqrt{3}x + 3)]Solving the quadratic equation ( x^2 - 4sqrt{3}x - 12 = 0 ), we get the roots:[x = 2sqrt{3} pm 2sqrt{6}]Substituting back to find ( y ), we get the points ( A = (2sqrt{3} + 2sqrt{6}, 9 + 6sqrt{2}) ) and ( B = (2sqrt{3} - 2sqrt{6}, 9 - 6sqrt{2}) ).The midpoint ( M ) of ( AB ) is:[M = left( frac{(2sqrt{3} + 2sqrt{6}) + (2sqrt{3} - 2sqrt{6})}{2}, frac{(9 + 6sqrt{2}) + (9 - 6sqrt{2})}{2} right) = (2sqrt{3}, 9)]The distance ( |PM| ) is calculated as:[|PM| = sqrt{(2sqrt{3} - 2sqrt{3})^2 + (9 - 6)^2} = sqrt{0 + 9} = 3]Final answers:(1) The ordinary equation of line ( l ) is (boxed{y = sqrt{3}x + 3}) and the rectangular coordinate equation of curve ( C ) is (boxed{x^2 = 4y}).(2) The value of ( |PM| ) is (boxed{3}).]

🔑:(1) The given parametric equation of line l is begin{cases} x= frac {1}{2}t y=3+ frac { sqrt {3}}{2}t end{cases} (t text{ is the parameter}) . We can rewrite y in terms of x by substituting t = 2x into the equation for y, which gives y = 3 + sqrt{3}x. This is the ordinary equation of line l.For curve C, we start with the polar coordinate equation ρcos ^{2}θ=4sin θ. Recall that in polar coordinates, x = ρcos θ and y = ρsin θ. Using these relationships, we can rewrite the equation as:x^2 = 4y(2) To find the intersection points of line l and curve C, we substitute the equation of line l into the equation of curve C. This gives us:x^2 - 4sqrt{3}x - 12 = 0Let A(x_1,y_1) and B(x_2,y_2) be the intersection points. The midpoint M of AB can be calculated using the midpoint formula, which gives M(2sqrt{3}, 9).Now, we need to find point P in rectangular coordinates. Given its polar coordinates (4 sqrt {3}, frac {π}{3}), we can use the relationships x = ρcos θ and y = ρsin θ to find that P(2sqrt{3}, 6).Finally, to find the distance |PM|, we use the distance formula:|PM| = sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2} = sqrt{(2sqrt{3} - 2sqrt{3})^2 + (6 - 9)^2} = boxed{3}

Mathematical Modeling: - Let the side length of the flag be 1 (unit square). - Let ( w ) be the distance from the diagonal to the edge of the strip. Each strip has a width of ( 2w sqrt{2} ).3. Area Calculations: - The area of one diagonal strip is ( 2wsqrt{2} - 2w^2 ). - The total area of both strips (without overlap) is ( 2 times (2wsqrt{2} - 2w^2) = 4wsqrt{2} - 4w^2 ). - The overlapping area (blue square) is ( 4w^2 ).4. 🔑:To find the probability that you do not eat a green candy immediately after eating a red candy, we need to consider the different ways in which the candies can be eaten. We will use combinatorial methods to calculate the probabilities for each case and then sum them up.1. Total number of ways to choose 5 candies out of 12: [ binom{12}{5} ]2. Case 1: 5 green candies and 0 red candies: [ frac{binom{8}{5} cdot binom{4}{0}}{binom{12}{5}} cdot frac{1}{binom{5}{0}} = frac{binom{8}{5}}{binom{12}{5}} ]3. Case 2: 4 green candies and 1 red candy: [ frac{binom{8}{4} cdot binom{4}{1}}{binom{12}{5}} cdot frac{1}{binom{5}{1}} = frac{binom{8}{4} cdot binom{4}{1}}{binom{12}{5} cdot 5} ]4. Case 3: 3 green candies and 2 red candies: [ frac{binom{8}{3} cdot binom{4}{2}}{binom{12}{5}} cdot frac{1}{binom{5}{2}} = frac{binom{8}{3} cdot binom{4}{2}}{binom{12}{5} cdot 10} ]5. Case 4: 2 green candies and 3 red candies: [ frac{binom{8}{2} cdot binom{4}{3}}{binom{12}{5}} cdot frac{1}{binom{5}{3}} = frac{binom{8}{2} cdot binom{4}{3}}{binom{12}{5} cdot 10} ]6. Case 5: 1 green candy and 4 red candies: [ frac{binom{8}{1} cdot binom{4}{4}}{binom{12}{5}} cdot frac{1}{binom{5}{4}} = frac{binom{8}{1} cdot binom{4}{4}}{binom{12}{5} cdot 5} ]7. Summing the probabilities: [ frac{binom{8}{5}}{binom{12}{5}} + frac{binom{8}{4} cdot binom{4}{1}}{binom{12}{5} cdot 5} + frac{binom{8}{3} cdot binom{4}{2}}{binom{12}{5} cdot 10} + frac{binom{8}{2} cdot binom{4}{3}}{binom{12}{5} cdot 10} + frac{binom{8}{1} cdot binom{4}{4}}{binom{12}{5} cdot 5} ]8. Simplifying the binomial coefficients: [ binom{8}{5} = binom{8}{3} = 56, quad binom{8}{4} = binom{8}{4} = 70, quad binom{8}{2} = binom{8}{6} = 28, quad binom{8}{1} = 8, quad binom{4}{1} = 4, quad binom{4}{2} = 6, quad binom{4}{3} = 4, quad binom{4}{4} = 1 ]9. Calculating each term: [ frac{56}{792} + frac{70 cdot 4}{792 cdot 5} + frac{56 cdot 6}{792 cdot 10} + frac{28 cdot 4}{792 cdot 10} + frac{8 cdot 1}{792 cdot 5} ]10. Simplifying each term: [ frac{56}{792} + frac{280}{3960} + frac{336}{7920} + frac{112}{7920} + frac{8}{3960} ]11. Summing the simplified terms: [ frac{56}{792} + frac{280}{3960} + frac{336}{7920} + frac{112}{7920} + frac{8}{3960} = frac{1}{5} ]12. Finding the relatively prime integers (m) and (n): [ frac{1}{5} implies m = 1, n = 5 ]13. Summing (m) and (n): [ m + n = 1 + 5 = 6 ]The final answer is (boxed{6})

Let's see:mn - 2m - 2n + 4 = (mn - 2m) - (2n - 4) = m(n - 2) - 2(n - 2) = (m - 2)(n - 2) = 0Ah, so (m - 2)(n - 2) = 0. Therefore, either m = 2 or n = 2.So, if either m or n is 2, then the quadratic factors. Let's check:If m = 2, then from m + n = a + 2, we have 2 + n = a + 2 ⇒ n = a. Then mn = 2a ⇒ 2 * a = 2a, which holds. Similarly, if n = 2, then m = a.Therefore, the quadratic factors as (x - 2)(x - a) ≤ 0. So, the roots are x = 2 and x = a. So, the solution set M is the interval between the smaller root and the larger root. Depending on the order of 2 and a, the interval will be [2, a] if a > 2 or [a, 2] if a < 2. If a = 2, then the quadratic is (x - 2)^2 ≤ 0, which only has the solution x = 2.So, summarizing:- If a > 2, then M = [2, a]- If a < 2, then M = [a, 2]- If a = 2, then M = {2}Now, we need M ⊆ A, where A is [1,4]. So, depending on the cases above, we need to ensure that the entire interval M is within [1,4].Let me consider each case separately.Case 1: a > 2Then M = [2, a]. For this interval to be a subset of [1,4], the upper bound a must be ≤ 4, and the lower bound 2 must be ≥ 1. Since 2 is already ≥ 1, the condition here is a ≤ 4. However, since a > 2 in this case, the range of a is 2 < a ≤ 4.Wait, but hold on. Also, a has to be such that the interval [2, a] is entirely within [1,4]. So, as long as a ≤ 4, since 2 is already within [1,4]. So, yes, if a is between 2 and 4, inclusive at 4, then [2, a] is inside [1,4]. If a > 4, then [2, a] would go beyond 4, which is not allowed. So, a must be ≤4. But since in this case a > 2, combining both conditions, a is in (2, 4].Case 2: a < 2Then M = [a, 2]. For this interval to be a subset of [1,4], the lower bound a must be ≥1, and the upper bound 2 is already ≤4. Since 2 is less than 4, the critical condition here is a ≥1. So, if a is between 1 and 2, then [a, 2] is within [1,4]. If a <1, then [a, 2] would start before 1, which is not allowed. So, for a <2, we need a ≥1. Therefore, in this case, a is in [1, 2).Case 3: a = 2Then M = {2}, which is trivially a subset of [1,4]. So, a =2 is allowed.Putting all cases together:- When a ≥1 and a ≤4, M is a subset of A.
